Rule Generator Message:
**Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an amino group (–NH2) tend to result in a high yield. This is likely due to the ability of these groups to interact with the metal center, enhancing catalyst performance. Examples include (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid and 5-aminopentanoic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl ring substituted with a hydroxyl group (–OH) and an additional functional group such as an alkyl chain or carboxylic acid tend to result in a high yield. This could be due to the electron-donating nature of the hydroxyl group, which may stabilize the catalyst. Examples include 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a sulfanyl group (–SH) and a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) tend to result in a high yield. The thiol group can form strong interactions with metal centers, potentially enhancing catalytic activity. Examples include 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in a low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of the pyridine ring may reduce the effectiveness of the catalyst. Examples include 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid and pyridine-3-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a formyl group (–CHO) tend to result in a low yield. The presence of the formyl group may lead to side reactions or deactivation of the catalyst. Examples include 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid and 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a nitro group (–NO2) on a phenyl ring tend to result in a high yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of the nitro group may facilitate the activation of the catalyst. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbons) without additional functional groups tend to result in a low yield. The lack of polar or reactive groups may lead to poor interaction with the catalyst. Examples include octanoic acid and 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers containing multiple ether linkages (–O–) tend to result in a low yield. The presence of multiple ether groups may lead to steric hindrance or reduced interaction with the catalyst. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

**End of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an amino group (–NH2) tend to result in a high yield. This combination likely enhances interaction with the metal center, improving catalyst performance. Examples include (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid and 5-aminopentanoic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl ring substituted with a hydroxyl group (–OH) and an additional functional group such as an alkyl chain or carboxylic acid tend to result in a high yield. The electron-donating nature of the hydroxyl group may stabilize the catalyst. Examples include 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a sulfanyl group (–SH) and a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) tend to result in a high yield. The thiol group can form strong interactions with metal centers, potentially enhancing catalytic activity. Examples include 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in a low yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of the pyridine ring may reduce the effectiveness of the catalyst. Examples include 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid and pyridine-3-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a formyl group (–CHO) tend to result in a low yield. The presence of the formyl group may lead to side reactions or deactivation of the catalyst. Examples include 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid and 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a nitro group (–NO2) on a phenyl ring tend to result in a high yield. The electron-withdrawing nature of the nitro group may facilitate the activation of the catalyst. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbons) without additional functional groups tend to result in a low yield. The lack of polar or reactive groups may lead to poor interaction with the catalyst. Examples include octanoic acid and 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers containing multiple ether linkages (–O–) tend to result in a low yield. The presence of multiple ether groups may lead to steric hindrance or reduced interaction with the catalyst. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield. The indole group can provide π-stacking interactions with the catalyst, enhancing stability and reactivity. An example is (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a benzofuran group tend to result in a high yield. The aromatic nature of benzofuran can enhance π-π interactions with the catalyst. An example is 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid.

**Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an amino group (–NH2) tend to result in a high yield, especially when the amino group is positioned such that it can form intramolecular hydrogen bonds, enhancing stability. Examples include (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid and 5-aminopentanoic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl ring substituted with a hydroxyl group (–OH) and an additional electron-donating group, such as an alkyl chain, tend to result in a high yield. The electron-donating nature of these groups can stabilize the catalyst. Examples include 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a sulfanyl group (–SH) and a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) tend to result in a high yield, particularly when the sulfanyl group is in a position to form strong interactions with metal centers. Examples include 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in a low yield unless the pyridine ring is substituted with electron-donating groups that can counteract the electron-withdrawing nature of the pyridine. Examples of low yield include 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a formyl group (–CHO) tend to result in a low yield unless accompanied by groups that can engage in stabilizing interactions, such as hydrogen bonding, which can mitigate the negative effects. Examples of low yield include 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a nitro group (–NO2) on a phenyl ring tend to result in a high yield due to the electron-withdrawing nature of the nitro group, which may facilitate catalyst activation. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbons) without additional functional groups tend to result in a low yield due to poor interaction with the catalyst. Examples include octanoic acid and 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers containing multiple ether linkages (–O–) tend to result in a low yield due to steric hindrance or reduced interaction with the catalyst. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield due to π-stacking interactions with the catalyst, enhancing stability and reactivity. An example is (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a benzofuran group tend to result in a high yield due to the aromatic nature of benzofuran, which can enhance π-π interactions with the catalyst. An example is 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring substituted with an electron-donating group such as an alkyl chain or amino group can result in a high yield by offsetting the electron-withdrawing effect of the pyridine. An example is pyridine-3-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and a formyl group (–CHO) on the same aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield due to potential side reactions or catalyst deactivation. Examples include 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a phenyl ring substituted with both electron-donating and electron-withdrawing groups can result in variable yields, depending on the balance of these effects. An example of high yield is 4-nitrobenzoic acid, while low yield includes 4-methylbenzoic acid.

**End of Rules**

    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an amino group (–NH2) tend to result in a high yield, especially when the amino group is positioned such that it can form intramolecular hydrogen bonds, enhancing stability. Examples include (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid and 5-aminopentanoic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl ring substituted with a hydroxyl group (–OH) and an additional electron-donating group, such as an alkyl chain, tend to result in a high yield. The electron-donating nature of these groups can stabilize the catalyst. Examples include 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a sulfanyl group (–SH) and a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) tend to result in a high yield, particularly when the sulfanyl group is in a position to form strong interactions with metal centers. Examples include 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in a low yield unless the pyridine ring is substituted with electron-donating groups that can counteract the electron-withdrawing nature of the pyridine. Examples of low yield include 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a formyl group (–CHO) tend to result in a low yield unless accompanied by groups that can engage in stabilizing interactions, such as hydrogen bonding, which can mitigate the negative effects. Examples of low yield include 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a nitro group (–NO2) on a phenyl ring tend to result in a high yield due to the electron-withdrawing nature of the nitro group, which may facilitate catalyst activation. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbons) without additional functional groups tend to result in a low yield due to poor interaction with the catalyst. Examples include octanoic acid and 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers containing multiple ether linkages (–O–) tend to result in a low yield due to steric hindrance or reduced interaction with the catalyst. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield due to π-stacking interactions with the catalyst, enhancing stability and reactivity. An example is (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a benzofuran group tend to result in a high yield due to the aromatic nature of benzofuran, which can enhance π-π interactions with the catalyst. An example is 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring substituted with an electron-donating group such as an alkyl chain or amino group can result in a high yield by offsetting the electron-withdrawing effect of the pyridine. An example is pyridine-3-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and a formyl group (–CHO) on the same aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield due to potential side reactions or catalyst deactivation. Examples include 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a phenyl ring substituted with both electron-donating and electron-withdrawing groups can result in variable yields, depending on the balance of these effects. An example of high yield is 4-nitrobenzoic acid, while low yield includes 4-methylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring substituted with an electron-donating group such as an alkyl chain or amino group can result in a high yield by offsetting the electron-withdrawing effect of the pyridine. An example is pyridine-3-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and a formyl group (–CHO) on the same aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield due to potential side reactions or catalyst deactivation. Examples include 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a phenyl ring substituted with both electron-donating and electron-withdrawing groups can result in variable yields, depending on the balance of these effects. An example of high yield is 4-nitrobenzoic acid, while low yield includes 4-methylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group (–OH) on a phenyl ring, especially when accompanied by other electron-withdrawing groups, tend to result in a low yield due to potential deactivation of the catalyst. An example is 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an additional electron-withdrawing group on the same aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield due to destabilization of the catalyst interaction. An example is 4-oxoheptanedioic acid.

**End of Rules**

    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an amino group (–NH2) in close proximity, allowing for intramolecular hydrogen bonding, tend to result in a high yield. This stabilizes the modifier and enhances its interaction with the catalyst. Examples include (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid and 5-aminopentanoic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl ring substituted with a hydroxyl group (–OH) and an additional electron-donating group, such as an alkyl chain, tend to result in a high yield. The electron-donating nature of these groups can stabilize the catalyst. Examples include 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a sulfanyl group (–SH) and a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) tend to result in a high yield, particularly when the sulfanyl group is positioned to form strong interactions with metal centers. Examples include 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in a low yield unless the pyridine ring is substituted with electron-donating groups that can counteract the electron-withdrawing nature of the pyridine. Examples of low yield include 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a formyl group (–CHO) tend to result in a low yield unless accompanied by groups that can engage in stabilizing interactions, such as hydrogen bonding, which can mitigate the negative effects. Examples of low yield include 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a nitro group (–NO2) on a phenyl ring tend to result in a high yield due to the electron-withdrawing nature of the nitro group, which may facilitate catalyst activation. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbons) without additional functional groups tend to result in a low yield due to poor interaction with the catalyst. Examples include octanoic acid and 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers containing multiple ether linkages (–O–) tend to result in a low yield due to steric hindrance or reduced interaction with the catalyst. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield due to π-stacking interactions with the catalyst, enhancing stability and reactivity. An example is (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a benzofuran group tend to result in a high yield due to the aromatic nature of benzofuran, which can enhance π-π interactions with the catalyst. An example is 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring substituted with an electron-donating group such as an alkyl chain or amino group can result in a high yield by offsetting the electron-withdrawing effect of the pyridine. An example is pyridine-3-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and a formyl group (–CHO) on the same aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield due to potential side reactions or catalyst deactivation. Examples include 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a phenyl ring substituted with both electron-donating and electron-withdrawing groups can result in variable yields, depending on the balance of these effects. An example of high yield is 4-nitrobenzoic acid, while low yield includes 4-methylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group (–OH) on a phenyl ring, especially when accompanied by other electron-withdrawing groups, tend to result in a low yield due to potential deactivation of the catalyst. An example is 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an additional electron-withdrawing group on the same aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield due to destabilization of the catalyst interaction. An example is 4-oxoheptanedioic acid.

The electron-donating nature of these groups can stabilize the catalyst. Examples include 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in a low yield unless the pyridine ring is substituted with electron-donating groups that can counteract the electron-withdrawing nature of the pyridine. Examples of low yield include 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a formyl group (–CHO) tend to result in a low yield unless accompanied by groups that can engage in stabilizing interactions, such as hydrogen bonding, which can mitigate the negative effects. Examples of low yield include 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a nitro group (–NO2) on a phenyl ring tend to result in a high yield due to the electron-withdrawing nature of the nitro group, which may facilitate catalyst activation. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

### Performance Evaluation of Current Rules

1. **Effectiveness of Current Rules:**
   - The current rules have shown some effectiveness, as indicated by the validation accuracy of 0.6. However, this is a decrease from previous iterations where accuracies were higher (e.g., 0.6571). This suggests that the rules may not be capturing all the necessary features or interactions to predict the loading accurately.

2. **Overfitting/Underfitting:**
   - The decrease in validation accuracy suggests potential overfitting to the training data. The model might be capturing noise rather than the underlying patterns, leading to poorer generalization on unseen data.

3. **Discrepancies Analysis:**
   - For molecules where the predicted loading is high but the experimental value is low, Rule 4 and Rule 1 often have high positive SHAP values. This indicates that these rules might be overestimating the loading.
   - Conversely, for molecules where the predicted loading is low but the experimental value is high, Rule 5 and Rule 11 often have high negative SHAP values, suggesting these rules might be underestimating the loading.

4. **Accuracy Improvements:**
   - The current accuracy metrics suggest that there is room for improvement. The decrease in accuracy from previous iterations indicates that the current set of rules may not be optimal and could benefit from refinement.

5. **Insights from SHAP Analysis:**
   - SHAP analysis indicates that Rule 1 and Rule 4 are contributing significantly to overestimations, while Rule 5 and Rule 11 are contributing to underestimations. This suggests that these rules need to be refined to better capture the true interactions affecting the loading.

### Recommendations for Improvement

1. **Refine Overestimated Rules:**
   - **Rule 1:** Consider revising the conditions under which the presence of both a carboxylic acid group and an amino group leads to high loading. It may be beneficial to include additional structural or electronic factors that influence this interaction.
   - **Rule 4:** Re-evaluate the impact of pyridine rings and their substitutions. Consider the specific positions and types of electron-donating groups that might counteract the electron-withdrawing nature of pyridine more effectively.

2. **Adjust Underestimated Rules:**
   - **Rule 5:** Investigate the conditions under which formyl groups lead to low yield. Consider additional stabilizing interactions that might mitigate the negative effects of formyl groups.
   - **Rule 11:** Reassess the impact of electron-donating groups on pyridine rings. It may be necessary to consider the strength and type of electron-donating groups that can effectively offset the electron-withdrawing effects.

3. **Improve Generalization:**
   - Introduce more diverse training data to help the model generalize better. This could involve including more examples of modifiers with varying functional groups and structural features.
   - Consider using regularization techniques to prevent overfitting and improve the model's ability to generalize to new data.

4. **Iterative Refinement:**
   - Use insights from SHAP analysis to iteratively refine the rules. Focus on rules with high SHAP values that consistently lead to incorrect predictions and adjust them based on observed patterns.
   - Continuously evaluate the effectiveness of rule changes by monitoring changes in validation accuracy and SHAP value distributions.

By implementing these recommendations, the rules can be refined to better capture the complex interactions affecting the loading of modifiers on catalysts, ultimately improving the model's predictive performance.

    Current_Rules:
    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an amino group (–NH2) with a spatial arrangement that allows for effective intramolecular hydrogen bonding tend to result in a high yield. This configuration stabilizes the modifier and enhances its interaction with the catalyst. Examples include (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid and 5-aminopentanoic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl ring substituted with a hydroxyl group (–OH) and an additional electron-donating group, such as an alkyl chain, tend to result in a high yield. The electron-donating nature of these groups can stabilize the catalyst. Examples include 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a sulfanyl group (–SH) and a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) tend to result in a high yield, particularly when the sulfanyl group is positioned to form strong interactions with metal centers. Examples include 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in a low yield unless the pyridine ring is substituted with electron-donating groups that can counteract the electron-withdrawing nature of the pyridine. Examples of low yield include 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a formyl group (–CHO) tend to result in a low yield unless accompanied by groups that can engage in stabilizing interactions, such as hydrogen bonding, which can mitigate the negative effects. Examples of low yield include 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a nitro group (–NO2) on a phenyl ring tend to result in a high yield due to the electron-withdrawing nature of the nitro group, which may facilitate catalyst activation. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbons) without additional functional groups tend to result in a low yield due to poor interaction with the catalyst. Examples include octanoic acid and 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers containing multiple ether linkages (–O–) tend to result in a low yield due to steric hindrance or reduced interaction with the catalyst. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield due to π-stacking interactions with the catalyst, enhancing stability and reactivity. An example is (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a benzofuran group tend to result in a high yield due to the aromatic nature of benzofuran, which can enhance π-π interactions with the catalyst. An example is 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring substituted with an electron-donating group such as an alkyl chain or amino group can result in a high yield by offsetting the electron-withdrawing effect of the pyridine. An example is pyridine-3-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and a formyl group (–CHO) on the same aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield due to potential side reactions or catalyst deactivation. Examples include 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a phenyl ring substituted with both electron-donating and electron-withdrawing groups can result in variable yields, depending on the balance of these effects. An example of high yield is 4-nitrobenzoic acid, while low yield includes 4-methylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group (–OH) on a phenyl ring, especially when accompanied by other electron-withdrawing groups, tend to result in a low yield due to potential deactivation of the catalyst. An example is 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an additional electron-withdrawing group on the same aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield due to destabilization of the catalyst interaction. An example is 4-oxoheptanedioic acid.

**Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an amino group (–NH2) with a spatial arrangement that allows for effective intramolecular hydrogen bonding, along with minimal steric hindrance, tend to result in a high yield. This configuration stabilizes the modifier and enhances its interaction with the catalyst. Examples include (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid and 5-aminopentanoic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl ring substituted with a hydroxyl group (–OH) and an additional electron-donating group, such as an alkyl chain, tend to result in a high yield. The electron-donating nature of these groups can stabilize the catalyst. Examples include 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a sulfanyl group (–SH) and a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) tend to result in a high yield, particularly when the sulfanyl group is positioned to form strong interactions with metal centers. Examples include 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in a low yield unless the pyridine ring is substituted with electron-donating groups that can counteract the electron-withdrawing nature of the pyridine. Examples of low yield include 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a formyl group (–CHO) tend to result in a low yield unless accompanied by groups that can engage in stabilizing interactions, such as hydrogen bonding or π-π interactions, which can mitigate the negative effects. Examples of low yield include 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a nitro group (–NO2) on a phenyl ring tend to result in a high yield due to the electron-withdrawing nature of the nitro group, which may facilitate catalyst activation. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbons) without additional functional groups tend to result in a low yield due to poor interaction with the catalyst. Examples include octanoic acid and 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers containing multiple ether linkages (–O–) tend to result in a low yield due to steric hindrance or reduced interaction with the catalyst. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield due to π-stacking interactions with the catalyst, enhancing stability and reactivity. An example is (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a benzofuran group tend to result in a high yield due to the aromatic nature of benzofuran, which can enhance π-π interactions with the catalyst. An example is 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring substituted with an electron-donating group such as an alkyl chain or amino group can result in a high yield by offsetting the electron-withdrawing effect of the pyridine. An example is pyridine-3-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and a formyl group (–CHO) on the same aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield due to potential side reactions or catalyst deactivation. Examples include 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a phenyl ring substituted with both electron-donating and electron-withdrawing groups can result in variable yields, depending on the balance of these effects. An example of high yield is 4-nitrobenzoic acid, while low yield includes 4-methylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group (–OH) on a phenyl ring, especially when accompanied by other electron-withdrawing groups, tend to result in a low yield due to potential deactivation of the catalyst. An example is 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an additional electron-withdrawing group on the same aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield due to destabilization of the catalyst interaction. An example is 4-oxoheptanedioic acid.

    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an amino group (–NH2) with a spatial arrangement that allows for effective intramolecular hydrogen bonding, along with minimal steric hindrance, tend to result in a high yield. This configuration stabilizes the modifier and enhances its interaction with the catalyst. Examples include (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid and 5-aminopentanoic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl ring substituted with a hydroxyl group (–OH) and an additional electron-donating group, such as an alkyl chain, tend to result in a high yield. The electron-donating nature of these groups can stabilize the catalyst. Examples include 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a sulfanyl group (–SH) and a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) tend to result in a high yield, particularly when the sulfanyl group is positioned to form strong interactions with metal centers. Examples include 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in a low yield unless the pyridine ring is substituted with electron-donating groups that can counteract the electron-withdrawing nature of the pyridine. Examples of low yield include 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a formyl group (–CHO) tend to result in a low yield unless accompanied by groups that can engage in stabilizing interactions, such as hydrogen bonding or π-π interactions, which can mitigate the negative effects. Examples of low yield include 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a nitro group (–NO2) on a phenyl ring tend to result in a high yield due to the electron-withdrawing nature of the nitro group, which may facilitate catalyst activation. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbons) without additional functional groups tend to result in a low yield due to poor interaction with the catalyst. Examples include octanoic acid and 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers containing multiple ether linkages (–O–) tend to result in a low yield due to steric hindrance or reduced interaction with the catalyst. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield due to π-stacking interactions with the catalyst, enhancing stability and reactivity. An example is (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a benzofuran group tend to result in a high yield due to the aromatic nature of benzofuran, which can enhance π-π interactions with the catalyst. An example is 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring substituted with an electron-donating group such as an alkyl chain or amino group can result in a high yield by offsetting the electron-withdrawing effect of the pyridine. An example is pyridine-3-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and a formyl group (–CHO) on the same aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield due to potential side reactions or catalyst deactivation. Examples include 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a phenyl ring substituted with both electron-donating and electron-withdrawing groups can result in variable yields, depending on the balance of these effects. An example of high yield is 4-nitrobenzoic acid, while low yield includes 4-methylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group (–OH) on a phenyl ring, especially when accompanied by other electron-withdrawing groups, tend to result in a low yield due to potential deactivation of the catalyst. An example is 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an additional electron-withdrawing group on the same aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield due to destabilization of the catalyst interaction. An example is 4-oxoheptanedioic acid.

Rule Generator Message:
**Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an amino group (–NH2) in a configuration that allows for effective intramolecular hydrogen bonding, without significant steric hindrance, tend to result in a high yield. This configuration stabilizes the modifier and enhances its interaction with the catalyst. Examples include (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid and 5-aminopentanoic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl ring substituted with a hydroxyl group (–OH) and an additional electron-donating group, such as an alkyl chain, tend to result in a high yield. The electron-donating nature of these groups can stabilize the catalyst. Examples include 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a sulfanyl group (–SH) and a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) tend to result in a high yield, particularly when the sulfanyl group is positioned to form strong interactions with metal centers. Examples include 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in a low yield unless the pyridine ring is substituted with electron-donating groups that can counteract the electron-withdrawing nature of the pyridine. Examples of low yield include 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a formyl group (–CHO) tend to result in a low yield unless accompanied by groups that can engage in stabilizing interactions, such as hydrogen bonding or π-π interactions, which can mitigate the negative effects. Examples of low yield include 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a nitro group (–NO2) on a phenyl ring tend to result in a high yield due to the electron-withdrawing nature of the nitro group, which may facilitate catalyst activation. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbons) without additional functional groups tend to result in a low yield due to poor interaction with the catalyst. Examples include octanoic acid and 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers containing multiple ether linkages (–O–) tend to result in a low yield due to steric hindrance or reduced interaction with the catalyst. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield due to π-stacking interactions with the catalyst, enhancing stability and reactivity. An example is (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a benzofuran group tend to result in a high yield due to the aromatic nature of benzofuran, which can enhance π-π interactions with the catalyst. An example is 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring substituted with an electron-donating group such as an alkyl chain or amino group can result in a high yield by offsetting the electron-withdrawing effect of the pyridine. An example is pyridine-3-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and a formyl group (–CHO) on the same aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield due to potential side reactions or catalyst deactivation. Examples include 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a phenyl ring substituted with both electron-donating and electron-withdrawing groups can result in variable yields, depending on the balance of these effects. An example of high yield is 4-nitrobenzoic acid, while low yield includes 4-methylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group (–OH) on a phenyl ring, especially when accompanied by other electron-withdrawing groups, tend to result in a low yield due to potential deactivation of the catalyst. An example is 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an additional electron-withdrawing group on the same aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield due to destabilization of the catalyst interaction. An example is 4-oxoheptanedioic acid.

**End of Rules**

ML Commenter Message:
### Performance Evaluation of Current Rules

1. **Effectiveness of Current Rules:**
   - The current rules seem to have mixed effectiveness. The validation accuracy has fluctuated between 0.57 and 0.66 in previous iterations, but the latest accuracy is 0.54, which is lower than the previous best. This suggests that the rules may not be consistently capturing the underlying patterns in the data.

2. **Overfitting/Underfitting Assessment:**
   - The decrease in validation accuracy suggests potential overfitting, where the model may be too tailored to the training data and not generalizing well to new data. The consistent use of rules without significant changes might also indicate underfitting, where the model is not complex enough to capture the data's nuances.

3. **Discrepancies Analysis:**
   - For molecules where the predicted loading is high but the experimental value is low, Rule 1 often has a high positive SHAP value, indicating it might be overestimating the loading. Conversely, for molecules where the predicted loading is low but the experimental value is high, Rule 5 and Rule 7 frequently have high negative SHAP values, suggesting they might be underestimating the loading.

4. **Accuracy Improvements:**
   - The current accuracy metrics suggest that there is room for improvement. The validation accuracy has not consistently improved, indicating that the rules may not be effectively capturing the necessary features for accurate predictions.

5. **Insights from SHAP Analysis:**
   - The SHAP analysis highlights specific rules that contribute significantly to the predictions. For instance, Rule 1 often has a high positive SHAP value, suggesting it might be too aggressive in predicting high loadings. Similarly, Rule 5 and Rule 7 have high negative SHAP values, indicating they might be too conservative.

### Recommendations for Improvement

1. **Refine Overestimated Loadings:**
   - Re-evaluate Rule 1, which frequently has high positive SHAP values for overestimated loadings. Consider adding conditions or constraints to this rule to prevent overestimation.

    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an amino group (–NH2) in a configuration that allows for effective intramolecular hydrogen bonding, without significant steric hindrance, tend to result in a high yield. This configuration stabilizes the modifier and enhances its interaction with the catalyst. Examples include (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid and 5-aminopentanoic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl ring substituted with a hydroxyl group (–OH) and an additional electron-donating group, such as an alkyl chain, tend to result in a high yield. The electron-donating nature of these groups can stabilize the catalyst. Examples include 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a sulfanyl group (–SH) and a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) tend to result in a high yield, particularly when the sulfanyl group is positioned to form strong interactions with metal centers. Examples include 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in a low yield unless the pyridine ring is substituted with electron-donating groups that can counteract the electron-withdrawing nature of the pyridine. Examples of low yield include 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a formyl group (–CHO) tend to result in a low yield unless accompanied by groups that can engage in stabilizing interactions, such as hydrogen bonding or π-π interactions, which can mitigate the negative effects. Examples of low yield include 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a nitro group (–NO2) on a phenyl ring tend to result in a high yield due to the electron-withdrawing nature of the nitro group, which may facilitate catalyst activation. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbons) without additional functional groups tend to result in a low yield due to poor interaction with the catalyst. Examples include octanoic acid and 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers containing multiple ether linkages (–O–) tend to result in a low yield due to steric hindrance or reduced interaction with the catalyst. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield due to π-stacking interactions with the catalyst, enhancing stability and reactivity. An example is (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a benzofuran group tend to result in a high yield due to the aromatic nature of benzofuran, which can enhance π-π interactions with the catalyst. An example is 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring substituted with an electron-donating group such as an alkyl chain or amino group can result in a high yield by offsetting the electron-withdrawing effect of the pyridine. An example is pyridine-3-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and a formyl group (–CHO) on the same aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield due to potential side reactions or catalyst deactivation. Examples include 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a phenyl ring substituted with both electron-donating and electron-withdrawing groups can result in variable yields, depending on the balance of these effects. An example of high yield is 4-nitrobenzoic acid, while low yield includes 4-methylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group (–OH) on a phenyl ring, especially when accompanied by other electron-withdrawing groups, tend to result in a low yield due to potential deactivation of the catalyst. An example is 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an additional electron-withdrawing group on the same aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield due to destabilization of the catalyst interaction. An example is 4-oxoheptanedioic acid.

**End of Rules**---------------------------------------------------------------

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an amino group (–NH2) in a configuration that allows for effective intramolecular hydrogen bonding, without significant steric hindrance, tend to result in a high yield. This configuration stabilizes the modifier and enhances its interaction with the catalyst. Examples include (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid and 5-aminopentanoic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl ring substituted with a hydroxyl group (–OH) and an additional electron-donating group, such as an alkyl chain, tend to result in a high yield. The electron-donating nature of these groups can stabilize the catalyst. Examples include 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a sulfanyl group (–SH) and a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) tend to result in a high yield, particularly when the sulfanyl group is positioned to form strong interactions with metal centers. Examples include 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in a low yield unless the pyridine ring is substituted with electron-donating groups that can counteract the electron-withdrawing nature of the pyridine. Examples of low yield include 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a formyl group (–CHO) tend to result in a low yield unless accompanied by groups that can engage in stabilizing interactions, such as hydrogen bonding or π-π interactions, which can mitigate the negative effects. Examples of low yield include 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a nitro group (–NO2) on a phenyl ring tend to result in a high yield due to the electron-withdrawing nature of the nitro group, which may facilitate catalyst activation. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbons) without additional functional groups tend to result in a low yield due to poor interaction with the catalyst. Examples include octanoic acid and 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers containing multiple ether linkages (–O–) tend to result in a low yield due to steric hindrance or reduced interaction with the catalyst. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield due to π-stacking interactions with the catalyst, enhancing stability and reactivity. An example is (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a benzofuran group tend to result in a high yield due to the aromatic nature of benzofuran, which can enhance π-π interactions with the catalyst. An example is 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring substituted with an electron-donating group such as an alkyl chain or amino group can result in a high yield by offsetting the electron-withdrawing effect of the pyridine. An example is pyridine-3-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and a formyl group (–CHO) on the same aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield due to potential side reactions or catalyst deactivation. Examples include 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a phenyl ring substituted with both electron-donating and electron-withdrawing groups can result in variable yields, depending on the balance of these effects. An example of high yield is 4-nitrobenzoic acid, while low yield includes 4-methylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group (–OH) on a phenyl ring, especially when accompanied by other electron-withdrawing groups, tend to result in a low yield due to potential deactivation of the catalyst. An example is 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an additional electron-withdrawing group on the same aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield due to destabilization of the catalyst interaction. An example is 4-oxoheptanedioic acid.

    Current_Rules:
    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an amino group (–NH2) in a configuration that allows for effective intramolecular hydrogen bonding, without significant steric hindrance, tend to result in a high yield. This configuration stabilizes the modifier and enhances its interaction with the catalyst. Examples include (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid and 5-aminopentanoic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl ring substituted with a hydroxyl group (–OH) and an additional electron-donating group, such as an alkyl chain, tend to result in a high yield. The electron-donating nature of these groups can stabilize the catalyst. Examples include 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a sulfanyl group (–SH) and a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) tend to result in a high yield, particularly when the sulfanyl group is positioned to form strong interactions with metal centers. Examples include 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in a low yield unless the pyridine ring is substituted with electron-donating groups that can counteract the electron-withdrawing nature of the pyridine. Examples of low yield include 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a formyl group (–CHO) tend to result in a low yield unless accompanied by groups that can engage in stabilizing interactions, such as hydrogen bonding or π-π interactions, which can mitigate the negative effects. Examples of low yield include 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a nitro group (–NO2) on a phenyl ring tend to result in a high yield due to the electron-withdrawing nature of the nitro group, which may facilitate catalyst activation. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbons) without additional functional groups tend to result in a low yield due to poor interaction with the catalyst. Examples include octanoic acid and 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers containing multiple ether linkages (–O–) tend to result in a low yield due to steric hindrance or reduced interaction with the catalyst. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield due to π-stacking interactions with the catalyst, enhancing stability and reactivity. An example is (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a benzofuran group tend to result in a high yield due to the aromatic nature of benzofuran, which can enhance π-π interactions with the catalyst. An example is 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring substituted with an electron-donating group such as an alkyl chain or amino group can result in a high yield by offsetting the electron-withdrawing effect of the pyridine. An example is pyridine-3-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and a formyl group (–CHO) on the same aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield due to potential side reactions or catalyst deactivation. Examples include 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a phenyl ring substituted with both electron-donating and electron-withdrawing groups can result in variable yields, depending on the balance of these effects. An example of high yield is 4-nitrobenzoic acid, while low yield includes 4-methylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group (–OH) on a phenyl ring, especially when accompanied by other electron-withdrawing groups, tend to result in a low yield due to potential deactivation of the catalyst. An example is 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an additional electron-withdrawing group on the same aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield due to destabilization of the catalyst interaction. An example is 4-oxoheptanedioic acid.

**End of Rules**---------------------------------------------------------------

**Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an amino group (–NH2) in a configuration that allows for effective intramolecular hydrogen bonding, with minimal steric hindrance, tend to result in a high yield. This configuration stabilizes the modifier and enhances its interaction with the catalyst. Examples include (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid and 5-aminopentanoic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl ring substituted with a hydroxyl group (–OH) and an additional electron-donating group, such as an alkyl chain, tend to result in a high yield. The electron-donating nature of these groups can stabilize the catalyst. Examples include 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a sulfanyl group (–SH) and a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) tend to result in a high yield, particularly when the sulfanyl group is positioned to form strong interactions with metal centers. Examples include 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in a low yield unless the pyridine ring is substituted with electron-donating groups that can counteract the electron-withdrawing nature of the pyridine. Examples of low yield include 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a formyl group (–CHO) tend to result in a low yield unless accompanied by groups that can engage in stabilizing interactions, such as hydrogen bonding or π-π interactions, which can mitigate the negative effects. Examples of low yield include 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a nitro group (–NO2) on a phenyl ring tend to result in a high yield due to the electron-withdrawing nature of the nitro group, which may facilitate catalyst activation. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbons) without additional functional groups tend to result in a low yield due to poor interaction with the catalyst. Examples include octanoic acid and 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers containing multiple ether linkages (–O–) tend to result in a low yield due to steric hindrance or reduced interaction with the catalyst. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield due to π-stacking interactions with the catalyst, enhancing stability and reactivity. An example is (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a benzofuran group tend to result in a high yield due to the aromatic nature of benzofuran, which can enhance π-π interactions with the catalyst. An example is 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring substituted with an electron-donating group such as an alkyl chain or amino group can result in a high yield by offsetting the electron-withdrawing effect of the pyridine. An example is pyridine-3-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and a formyl group (–CHO) on the same aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield due to potential side reactions or catalyst deactivation. Examples include 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a phenyl ring substituted with both electron-donating and electron-withdrawing groups can result in variable yields, depending on the balance of these effects. An example of high yield is 4-nitrobenzoic acid, while low yield includes 4-methylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group (–OH) on a phenyl ring, especially when accompanied by other electron-withdrawing groups, tend to result in a low yield due to potential deactivation of the catalyst. An example is 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an additional electron-withdrawing group on the same aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield due to destabilization of the catalyst interaction. An example is 4-oxoheptanedioic acid.

### Performance Evaluation of Current Rules

1. **Effectiveness of Current Rules:**
   - The current rules have shown some effectiveness, as indicated by the validation accuracy of 0.6286. However, this is a slight improvement from previous iterations, suggesting that while the rules are capturing some patterns, there is still room for improvement.

2. **Overfitting/Underfitting Assessment:**
   - The consistent validation accuracy across iterations suggests that the model is not significantly overfitting. However, the relatively low accuracy indicates potential underfitting, where the rules may not be capturing all the necessary features to accurately predict the modifier loading.

3. **Discrepancies Analysis:**
   - For molecules where the predicted loading is high but the experimental value is low, Rule 1 often has a high positive SHAP value, indicating it might be overestimating the loading. Conversely, for molecules where the predicted loading is low but the experimental value is high, Rule 5 and Rule 7 often have high negative SHAP values, suggesting they might be underestimating the loading.

4. **Accuracy Improvements:**
   - The slight improvement in accuracy suggests that the current rules are moving in the right direction, but the progress is slow. This indicates that while the rules are somewhat effective, they may need further refinement to capture more complex interactions.

5. **Insights from SHAP Analysis:**
   - SHAP analysis indicates that Rule 1 is frequently contributing to overestimated loadings, while Rule 5 and Rule 7 contribute to underestimated loadings. This suggests that these rules may need to be adjusted to better align with experimental data.

### Recommendations for Improvement

1. **Refine Rule 1:**
   - Rule 1, which deals with modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group and an amino group, may need to be more specific about the configurations that lead to high yield. Consider adding conditions related to steric hindrance or specific intramolecular interactions that could affect the yield.

2. **Adjust Rule 5 and Rule 7:**
   - Rule 5, which involves modifiers with a formyl group, and Rule 7, which involves long aliphatic chains, should be revisited. Consider incorporating additional conditions or exceptions that account for stabilizing interactions that could lead to higher yields than currently predicted.

3. **Improve Generalization:**
   - To address potential underfitting, consider expanding the feature set to include additional chemical properties or interactions that may influence modifier loading. This could involve incorporating more complex molecular descriptors or considering the effects of solvent or temperature.

4. **Iterative Refinement:**
   - Use insights from SHAP analysis to iteratively refine the rules. For example, if a rule consistently shows high positive or negative SHAP values for incorrect predictions, investigate the underlying assumptions of that rule and adjust accordingly.

5. **Evaluate Rule Interactions:**
   - Consider the interactions between different rules and how they might collectively influence predictions. It may be beneficial to develop composite rules that consider multiple features simultaneously, rather than evaluating each feature in isolation.

By implementing these recommendations, the rules can be refined to improve their predictive accuracy and better align with experimental observations. This iterative process will help in achieving a more robust and generalizable model.

    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an amino group (–NH2) in a configuration that allows for effective intramolecular hydrogen bonding, with minimal steric hindrance, tend to result in a high yield. This configuration stabilizes the modifier and enhances its interaction with the catalyst. Examples include (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid and 5-aminopentanoic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl ring substituted with a hydroxyl group (–OH) and an additional electron-donating group, such as an alkyl chain, tend to result in a high yield. The electron-donating nature of these groups can stabilize the catalyst. Examples include 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a sulfanyl group (–SH) and a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) tend to result in a high yield, particularly when the sulfanyl group is positioned to form strong interactions with metal centers. Examples include 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in a low yield unless the pyridine ring is substituted with electron-donating groups that can counteract the electron-withdrawing nature of the pyridine. Examples of low yield include 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a formyl group (–CHO) tend to result in a low yield unless accompanied by groups that can engage in stabilizing interactions, such as hydrogen bonding or π-π interactions, which can mitigate the negative effects. Examples of low yield include 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a nitro group (–NO2) on a phenyl ring tend to result in a high yield due to the electron-withdrawing nature of the nitro group, which may facilitate catalyst activation. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbons) without additional functional groups tend to result in a low yield due to poor interaction with the catalyst. Examples include octanoic acid and 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers containing multiple ether linkages (–O–) tend to result in a low yield due to steric hindrance or reduced interaction with the catalyst. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield due to π-stacking interactions with the catalyst, enhancing stability and reactivity. An example is (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a benzofuran group tend to result in a high yield due to the aromatic nature of benzofuran, which can enhance π-π interactions with the catalyst. An example is 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring substituted with an electron-donating group such as an alkyl chain or amino group can result in a high yield by offsetting the electron-withdrawing effect of the pyridine. An example is pyridine-3-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and a formyl group (–CHO) on the same aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield due to potential side reactions or catalyst deactivation. Examples include 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a phenyl ring substituted with both electron-donating and electron-withdrawing groups can result in variable yields, depending on the balance of these effects. An example of high yield is 4-nitrobenzoic acid, while low yield includes 4-methylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group (–OH) on a phenyl ring, especially when accompanied by other electron-withdrawing groups, tend to result in a low yield due to potential deactivation of the catalyst. An example is 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an additional electron-withdrawing group on the same aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield due to destabilization of the catalyst interaction. An example is 4-oxoheptanedioic acid.

**End of Rules**---------------------------------------------------------------

Rule Generator Message:
**Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an amino group (–NH2) in a configuration that allows for effective intramolecular hydrogen bonding, such as in a five or six-membered ring, tend to result in a high yield. This configuration stabilizes the modifier and enhances its interaction with the catalyst. Examples include (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid and 5-aminopentanoic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl ring substituted with a hydroxyl group (–OH) and an additional electron-donating group, such as an alkyl chain, tend to result in a high yield. The electron-donating nature of these groups can stabilize the catalyst. Examples include 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a sulfanyl group (–SH) and a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) tend to result in a high yield, particularly when the sulfanyl group is positioned to form strong interactions with metal centers. Examples include 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in a low yield unless the pyridine ring is substituted with electron-donating groups that can counteract the electron-withdrawing nature of the pyridine. Examples of low yield include 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a formyl group (–CHO) tend to result in a low yield unless accompanied by groups that can engage in stabilizing interactions, such as hydrogen bonding or π-π interactions, which can mitigate the negative effects. Examples of low yield include 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a nitro group (–NO2) on a phenyl ring tend to result in a high yield due to the electron-withdrawing nature of the nitro group, which may facilitate catalyst activation. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbons) without additional functional groups tend to result in a low yield due to poor interaction with the catalyst. However, if the chain includes polar or charged groups that enhance solubility or binding, the yield can be high. Examples of low yield include octanoic acid and 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers containing multiple ether linkages (–O–) tend to result in a low yield due to steric hindrance or reduced interaction with the catalyst. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield due to π-stacking interactions with the catalyst, enhancing stability and reactivity. An example is (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a benzofuran group tend to result in a high yield due to the aromatic nature of benzofuran, which can enhance π-π interactions with the catalyst. An example is 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring substituted with an electron-donating group such as an alkyl chain or amino group can result in a high yield by offsetting the electron-withdrawing effect of the pyridine. An example is pyridine-3-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and a formyl group (–CHO) on the same aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield due to potential side reactions or catalyst deactivation. Examples include 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a phenyl ring substituted with both electron-donating and electron-withdrawing groups can result in variable yields, depending on the balance of these effects. An example of high yield is 4-nitrobenzoic acid, while low yield includes 4-methylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group (–OH) on a phenyl ring, especially when accompanied by other electron-withdrawing groups, tend to result in a low yield due to potential deactivation of the catalyst. An example is 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an additional electron-withdrawing group on the same aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield due to destabilization of the catalyst interaction. An example is 4-oxoheptanedioic acid.

Although the leverage values are not very high, they are positive, which is a good sign of the rules' effectiveness.

ML Commenter Message:
### Performance Evaluation of Current Rules

1. **Effectiveness of Current Rules:**
   - The current rules have shown some effectiveness, as indicated by the validation accuracy of 0.6. However, this is not a significant improvement over previous iterations, suggesting that the rules may not be capturing all the necessary features for accurate predictions.

2. **Overfitting/Underfitting:**
   - The consistent validation accuracy across iterations suggests that the model may be underfitting. The rules might not be complex enough to capture the nuances of the data, or they might be missing critical features that influence the loading of modifiers on catalysts.

3. **Discrepancies Analysis:**
   - For molecules where the predicted loading is high but the experimental value is low, Rule 4 and Rule 5 often have high positive SHAP values, indicating they might be overestimating the loading.
   - Conversely, for molecules where the predicted loading is low but the experimental value is high, Rule 1 and Rule 9 often have high negative SHAP values, suggesting they might be underestimating the loading.

4. **Accuracy Improvements:**
   - The accuracy metrics suggest limited improvement potential with the current rules. The validation accuracy has not significantly increased, indicating that the rules may need substantial refinement or additional rules may be necessary to capture more complex interactions.

5. **Insights from SHAP Analysis:**
   - SHAP analysis indicates that certain rules, such as Rule 4 and Rule 5, might be contributing to overestimated loadings, while Rule 1 and Rule 9 might be contributing to underestimated loadings. This suggests a need to refine these rules to better align with experimental data.

### Recommendations for Improvement

1. **Refine Overestimated Loading Rules:**
   - **Rule 4:** Consider revising the conditions under which pyridine rings result in low yield. Explore additional factors that might influence the interaction of pyridine with the catalyst.
   - **Rule 5:** Re-evaluate the impact of formyl groups and consider additional stabilizing interactions that might mitigate their negative effects.

2. **Adjust Underestimated Loading Rules:**
   - **Rule 1:** Reassess the conditions for intramolecular hydrogen bonding and its impact on loading. Consider additional structural features that might enhance this interaction.
   - **Rule 9:** Investigate the conditions under which indole groups enhance loading and consider additional factors that might influence this interaction.

3. **Improve Generalization:**
   - Introduce new rules or modify existing ones to capture more complex interactions, such as synergistic effects between multiple functional groups.
   - Consider incorporating additional data or features that might provide more context for the rules, such as solvent effects or temperature conditions.

4. **Iterative Refinement:**
   - Use insights from SHAP analysis to iteratively refine the rules. Focus on rules with consistently high positive or negative SHAP values and adjust them to better align with experimental outcomes.
   - Continuously evaluate the effectiveness of rule modifications by monitoring changes in validation accuracy and SHAP value distributions.

By addressing these areas, the rules can be refined to improve their predictive accuracy and better capture the complex interactions that influence the loading of modifiers on catalysts.

    **Start of Rules**

- **Rule 1**: Modifiers containing both a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an amino group (–NH2) in a configuration that allows for effective intramolecular hydrogen bonding, such as in a five or six-membered ring, tend to result in a high yield. This configuration stabilizes the modifier and enhances its interaction with the catalyst. Examples include (2S)-2-aminopentanedioic acid and 5-aminopentanoic acid.

- **Rule 2**: Modifiers with a phenyl ring substituted with a hydroxyl group (–OH) and an additional electron-donating group, such as an alkyl chain, tend to result in a high yield. The electron-donating nature of these groups can stabilize the catalyst. Examples include 5-(4-hydroxyphenyl)pentanoic acid.

- **Rule 3**: Modifiers containing a sulfanyl group (–SH) and a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) tend to result in a high yield, particularly when the sulfanyl group is positioned to form strong interactions with metal centers. Examples include 3-sulfanylpropanoic acid.

- **Rule 4**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring and a carboxylic acid group tend to result in a low yield unless the pyridine ring is substituted with electron-donating groups that can counteract the electron-withdrawing nature of the pyridine. Examples of low yield include 4-pyridin-4-ylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 5**: Modifiers with a formyl group (–CHO) tend to result in a low yield unless accompanied by groups that can engage in stabilizing interactions, such as hydrogen bonding or π-π interactions, which can mitigate the negative effects. Examples of low yield include 5-formyl-2-hydroxybenzoic acid.

- **Rule 6**: Modifiers with a nitro group (–NO2) on a phenyl ring tend to result in a high yield due to the electron-withdrawing nature of the nitro group, which may facilitate catalyst activation. An example is 4-nitrobenzoic acid.

- **Rule 7**: Modifiers with a long aliphatic chain (more than 6 carbons) without additional functional groups tend to result in a low yield due to poor interaction with the catalyst. However, if the chain includes polar or charged groups that enhance solubility or binding, the yield can be high. Examples of low yield include octanoic acid and 7-hydroxyheptanoic acid.

- **Rule 8**: Modifiers containing multiple ether linkages (–O–) tend to result in a low yield due to steric hindrance or reduced interaction with the catalyst. An example is 2-[2-(2-methoxyethoxy)ethoxy]acetic acid.

- **Rule 9**: Modifiers with an indole group tend to result in a high yield due to π-stacking interactions with the catalyst, enhancing stability and reactivity. An example is (2R)-2-amino-3-(1H-indol-3-yl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 10**: Modifiers with a benzofuran group tend to result in a high yield due to the aromatic nature of benzofuran, which can enhance π-π interactions with the catalyst. An example is 1,3-dioxo-2-benzofuran-5-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 11**: Modifiers with a pyridine ring substituted with an electron-donating group such as an alkyl chain or amino group can result in a high yield by offsetting the electron-withdrawing effect of the pyridine. An example is pyridine-3-carboxylic acid.

- **Rule 12**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and a formyl group (–CHO) on the same aromatic ring tend to result in a low yield due to potential side reactions or catalyst deactivation. Examples include 4-formylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 13**: Modifiers with a phenyl ring substituted with both electron-donating and electron-withdrawing groups can result in variable yields, depending on the balance of these effects. An example of high yield is 4-nitrobenzoic acid, while low yield includes 4-methylbenzoic acid.

- **Rule 14**: Modifiers with a hydroxyl group (–OH) on a phenyl ring, especially when accompanied by other electron-withdrawing groups, tend to result in a low yield due to potential deactivation of the catalyst. An example is 3-(4-hydroxyphenyl)propanoic acid.

- **Rule 15**: Modifiers with a carboxylic acid group (–COOH) and an additional electron-withdrawing group on the same aliphatic chain tend to result in a low yield due to destabilization of the catalyst interaction. An example is 4-oxoheptanedioic acid.
